Thanks for the very interesting video. Maybe the recurve penetrates farther because the arrow is going faster? I know that sounds obvious but an arrow's speed is a result of a continuous pressure against the nock by the string for a longer time.  A very high pull weight bow may only have a lot of pressure at the first moment of release and then not be able to move the string fast enough to maintain pressure against the nock. The total energy of the arrow is determined by how long the string can push the arrow and transfer energy. Imagine hitting a marble with a baseball bat, you will only be able to transfer energy for a very short time, compared to hitting a baseball.

These are recurve (reflex) bows. Developed by our ancestors, this is the Hun recurve bow. The bottom line is that it gives you much more thrust with a shorter draw than a long bow. Also, since we used it on horseback, it couldn't have been a huge bow. The other eastern peoples learned this form of bow from us, and the stirrup, without which you cannot shoot accurately from horseback while galloping, especially backwards. Today, a Hungarian archer can shoot at a distance of more than 1,000 meters with a bow he made using ancient techniques (except varnishing :), this is a world record. His name is JÃ³zsef MÃ³nus, USA, the state of Utah held the world record in 2023. Link:   https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=EBGfPo3hQW0

We used to do the same thing growing up ,this is what we used 1 half inch PVC pipe 1 3/4 inch PVC pipe put the half inch incide the 3/4 then cut notches about 1/4 of the way through the 3/4 inch. Then bend the pipe  using a person on ether side and a piece of steal suspension line ( single strand). The arrows we used duct tape for the fletching , aluminum tubing for the for the arrow shaft and take a file to the end get it as sharp as you can. You might not think this has much of a draw but if done right it has plenty. To those interested in/discussing recurves in the comments:  A recurve is formally defined as a bow where the string touches the belly of the limbs. If the limbs are curved but don't touch the belly, it's considered to be reflexed or flipped-tips. If the limbs are straight, it's a longbow.  It's complicated, but recurves don't technically store more energy than a longbow, they just effectively shorten the working limb (unless they're working recurves, which unravel as the bow is drawn, but these are exceptionally rare and finnicky). A 10in piece of pipe is more difficult to bend than a 15in pipe, and thus bending both the same amount will store more energy. This is where most of the actual power increase comes from when you recurve a longbow: it increases draw weight, but the same effect can be produced by just shortening the longbow instead, or making the bow to a higher draw weight.   However, at a certain length of bow, the angle between the string and limb exceeds 90deg, and two things happen: First, the string risks flying off the bow Second, the string begins to pull the limbs *out* instead of down. This is called stacking, and it strains the bow without increasing arrow speed.   This is where recurves are handy: they make it so that the string angle never exceeds 90deg, allowing for more bend to be gotten out of a shorter bow. However, most materials will break at this level of deflection when made with reasonable dimensions, so it doesn't really add much benefit.  If you recurve a bow which is long enough to not exceed 90deg of string angle, then the only benefit you can possibly reap is having a higher early draw weight and a slightly lower late draw weight. This is because, when the string contacts the belly of the bow when braced, it's effectively a shorter bow and thus harder to draw. As you pull back the string, it lifts off of the limbs and becomes a longer bow and easier to draw. This effect increases with the length and intensity of the recurve, but the effect is normally not very prominent.  Recurves have several weaknesses, which personally compel me to make exclusively longbows: Firstly, they're harder to make  Secondly, and most importantly, they're usually heavy, and heavy in the wrong place. In order to stay bent without breaking (or flexing and losing their bend over time), they need more material than longbows. Mass near the tips of a bow is the worst place for it: imagine a swinging a baseball bat with a 2lb weight at the handle. Then imagine moving that weight to the tip. It now takes considerably more effort to swing. The same thing happens to a bow: it gets slower and less efficient, negating any effect of recurving.  Third, they're louder and have the potential to twist out of alignment. When you shoot the bow, the string returns to brace. On a recurve, that means slapping the bow, which is quite loud. The heavy tips also return home with more force, which leads to uncomfortable vibration and additional noise/wear on the bow. This energy is wasted, and doesn't go into the arrow.  A longbow made to a proper length (there are equations) will be faster, quieter, and more pleasant to shoot than a recurve.  tl;dr: Recurves aren't actually more powerful than a longbow. They just fool you into thinking so, and all of their benefits are negated by making a slightly better designed longbow. They can actually make your bow slower and more uncomfortable.

yes, you do need to use a heavier arrow even to get a decent percentage of power out of a higher poundage bow , if you do not use a heavier arrow the arrow will be launched without even soaking in a decent amount of power thus yielding a result similar to what you see here. The second reason why recurve bow performs better is because it was designed to, it is an upgrade on the longbow. So two similar bow with the same poundage but different designs may lead to recurve bow performing better.
The point of bow construction is to make an arrow fly fast.  A heavy draw weight does not guarantee speed, it just means you can shoot a heavy arrow.  Expert bow makers fine tune their bows to snap back quickly, resulting in higher arrow velocity.

I did the math. The arrows were moving at 41fps.  We know that the string is 54 inches long because the distance between the notches was 58 inches(5 feet mines an inch on either end) and the string was 4 inches short of the second notch. using a frame where he had the bow at full draw, I calculated that the arrows were 32 inches long and it took 2 frames to travel its own length, so in .0667 seconds it travel 1.6 feet, giving us a final velocity of 41fps.

Just to let you know about the recurve bow. The one aspect of a recurve bow is that you have a more steady tension load throughout the entire draw. For instance, while the bow is sitting static you could have 35 pounds and at full draw you could have 45 pounds so roughly there would be a 40 pound exerted pressure on the arrow from when it is released, at your grip, until the arrow separates from the cord.  More importantly, you are at 35 pounds of pressure against the arrow when it leaves the cord.  A long bow starts at 20 pounds and will pull back at full draw to about 80 pounds.  Unfortunately, the average is not adding both and dividing by 2 because most of the draw weight at 80 pounds is in the last 2" of draw. So you start with 80 pounds of acceleration pressure against the arrow and then it decreases rapidly. Having this deceleration of acceleration causes the overall performance of the arrow to not penetrate as well. The best way to test is not into a target but to test the Feet/Second the arrow is traveling.   First side note,  Drawing back a recurve at 45 pounds, a person will normally have a full draw. As opposed to a 3/4 or 7/8, shaky arm draw, due to the fact that it is easier to pull back a full draw on the 45 pound recurve than it is on the 80 pound longbow.  Second side note, it seemed that the recurve hit more centered on to the target not moving the target as much.  This would be like catching a baseball stiff armed as opposed to using your elbow for recoil cushioning. The ball won't bruise your hand  And, the arrow won't penetrate as far into the target.The longbow seem to move the target because it was hit off center.  My suggestion to correct for this, put the target against a solid backstop.  I hope this helps.

Late response and by now you probably already know this but the reason re-curve bows (and the most modern answer to the issue compound bows) exist, is to get more bang for buck. higher force is not the only factor velocity of the arrow. how long and when you apply the force matter just as much. Your pvc "longbow like" bow applies a lot of force in the beginning but looses force quicker so the final velocity is a little less probably than that of the re-curve. Compound Bows are generally even more efficient than re-curve.

5:58 The reason why the 70# bow is not penetrating as much as the recurve is because the arrow is too light. If the arrow was actually heavier, the heavier draw bow will get it to penetrate more. It has to do with the spine and physics of your arrow AND the bow itself. That is why you never shoot light arrows with a heavy draw bow. The arrow is bending a lot more as it is being fired compared to the recurve bow. Archers will figure out what spine arrow they need for the pound draw of the bow. Heavy arrows have to be shot by heavy bows in order to be efficient.  As you were shooting those arrows, eli, the arrows were not flying true. they were angled downward which is not good.
bowyer bible vol 1 would explain alot  basicly it is possible to have high poundage bows but have no actull power behind them i had a 55lb bow i made ((that was what the draw weight said)) be as weak as a 10lbs kids bow and the reason why is that it had very thick limbs wich made it harder to pull but eats up the potencial energy on the release  you got to get a good balance between thickness/thiness of limbs and draw weight since your 75lb bow is 1 inch thick  chances are the thickness is eating up the potencial energy but try to get a copy of bowyer bible vol 1 and it explain things alot better then i do.......also bow design also has alot to do with efficientcy the correct design can give you higher speeds for less draw weight BUT ALSO the tips will act like cams ((the round wheels on a compound bow)) giving the bow a natrual let off ((meaning you going to pull it and makeing up some numbers half way through the pull it might reach 50lbs but once you pull it all the way you be holding 45-35 lbs depending on how much let off it provides and by the way the let off will still have the streangth potencial of 50lbs even though its 45-35lbs

Recurve looks like it bends and stores more kinetic energy better than the monster longbow. Just my observation from the last two clips in comparison.
The recurve bow, in your case, works because the curved bits at the end hold the string physically further from your body when the bow is drawn.  Watch the serious British longbow guys, and youâ€™ll note that they have this awkward lean thing they do.  They do it because with the bow that long, the angle of the string intersects your body early, before you get to the bowâ€™s full strength if you donâ€™t.   Look at the video of you shooting the bow and watch the bottom half of the string.  The string hits your body at the same angle on both bows, which makes sense, your draw length is the same on both.  But, because with the longbow the string is in a different relation to the angle of the recurve bow, that means you arenâ€™t getting the full draw on the longbow.  Which is why Native American hunting bows averaged around 45 pounds.  Itâ€™s a good combination of being able to draw and loose quickly and hitting hard enough to wound or kill a deer sized target.  You donâ€™t need to be real strong to draw it, so anyone in the family can use it, and you donâ€™t have to be standing at full height and leaning forward to get use out of it, so you have more options in how to approach the prey.   45 pounds and about the size of that recurve is just damned convenient for hunting in brush, and itâ€™s about as powerful as you can get for a median height person using simple techniques and natural materials without just scaling it bigger.   But if you want to scale it bigger and get full use out of that longbowâ€™s power, watch those Welshmen go at it for a while.
hi, whatÂ´s the diameter of the highway markers?, can i use instead fiber glass rods?. Excellent video
Recurve bows have a longer draw length because it goes till it hits the wood accelerating the arrow over a longer distance therefore upping speed for a lighter draw weight... it's like saying 50 pounds over 1 foot of acceleration or 40 pounds applied to accelerate over a foot and a half
Higher change of rate of acceleration...
The recurve adds speed because the ends act like levers different from the central "main" curve. As important, they add a more continuous (longer) acceleration just as the energy of the main curve is spent. They are "whipped" like the end of  leather whip right at the end of the main curves release of tension. Your long bows have power to launch heavier arrows, but for speed - recurve.

the muzzle energy of an arrow is actually the integral of string's pushing force times pushing distance, the pushing force is keep reducing along the distance. The 45lb recurve bow's max draw force is lower, but the force reduction along distance is more gentle and the pushing distance is longer, that makes the arrow more powerful then the 75lb monster bow

Recurve bows are designed to have more force in a smaller bow with a lower draw weight, so it stands to reason, a recurve bow with a 45 lbs draw weight might compare to a 60-80 lbs longbow. Due to the shape of the recurve, it provides a more effective storage of energy, and thereby a more efficient transfer of energy into the arrow. Therefore the subscriberâ€™s estimate may have been based on equivalent draw weight in an ordinary longbow, not actual draw weight.

Recurve does provide more benefit towards the draw/release. With wood recurve, the wood deforms when drawn and the additional strain of the recurve grants more "weight" than otherwise the stave of the bow could provide. I assume this carries over with most metallic bow staves (maybe) I have no idea if that would would on pipe so much... worth checking. I would guess it would have some effect, but that the actual effect would vary between bow stave materials. As I understand it, the recurve in a bow stave provides greater initial acceleration to the released projectile, therefor giving it more "weight". <-- Simplified.

Recurve acts like a coil spring at each end decreasing the recovery time of the nock of the string hence velocity at release is increased. The faster the nock recovers the faster the arrow will be moving and impact energy = mass of the arrow  x the velocity squared of that arrow. Hence an arrow weighing 280 grains fired by a mediaeval longbow travels at about 175 fps; from a commercial recurve that velocity is 225 fps; commercial compound (pulley) bows give 290 fps.   Impact energies Longbow arrow - about 1,200 foot pounds Recurve - about 2,000 foot pounds Compound - about 3,350 foot pounds For comparison a .22 LR (32 grain) has an impact energy at the muzzle of about 8,500 foot pounds

if the bow strung at 4" is 0 and the bow full drawn at 28" is 100, then the normal bow goes from 100 to 0 in a split second, lets say 0.2seconds, driven by the force of the draw, lets say 50lb. then we look at the recurve bow, with the same 50lb draw, it reaches zero in 0.1seconds. why? the arc of the normal bow drawn fully to 100 is 12"/30cm past 0 and must move that distance at 200kmph/120mph, while the recurve bow has 2, 3, or 4 arcs and each is respectively drawn back a fraction of that 12" distance; 3-6"/8-15cm, then for each arc of the recurve bow to move 3-6" at 120mph takes 1/2 the time of the normal bows 12" arc at the same speed. This in turn drives the arrow faster at a lower poundage draw weight.   hopefully that makes sense.

it might be the precision of the force you're dispensing the moment you release the draw string, that could affect the power directed to the arrow. the force probably disperses much more greatly with the long bow due to the much higher resistance and rigidity from the pvc pipe, which probably takes away from the amount of power directed to firing the arrow. with the recurve bow, the energy isn't wasted as much, so more of it is directed to the arrow for a more powerful and accurate shot (one end doesn't shift from the x direction as much, allowing for a straighter shot.)

This is three years old now but the answers to your questions.  Yes recurve bows are more efficient than a standard bow however the main reason you are not getting as much penetration with the 75lb bow is the assumption you made that getting close to the full draw must mean you are getting close to 75lb.  In actuality the power to draw ratio is not 1:1 but a logarithmic scale.  This means if you are just 3% off of the full draw you have far less than 72.75lb of draw.  You are probably only getting around 40lb of draw.

Materil in aarrow plz tell
In the test where the result for the large bow and the recurve bow I have an explanation. To start the advantage of the recurve design is the added flex it gives at the ends allowing for a longer draw. Additionally while bending the bow in this manner the direction of the force you apply to the string and the force the string translates to the bow has a more shallow angle to the arrow allowing for a better translation of power as you loose the string.  As for the large bow there's a good possibility that the actual bow is too straight meaning that the force allpied to the middle of the string and the angle of force applied to the edges of the bow is too shallow. Effectively this means that you are not only pulling the string back, but also pulling the edges towards you just to initialise the bend. If you would reduce the stiffness and weight at the edges of this bow you may feel that it's draw becomes easier and perhaps it's actual effective power is most likely closer to the recurve bow. Cheers.

Hey, Recurve Bows are more powerful because the extra bend on either end allows for more spring. You are no longer getting a bend from just the middle of the shaft; each end of the bow also bends. So basicallly, it's like having miniature bows on each end of your bow, but at lower draw weights. Because of that, the "bows" on the end reach their max  draw weights (Ã—2) before the actual main bend in the entire bow as a whole. This means the bow bends much farther, even though the actual distance the arrow travels remains the same.   Recap-  More spring from a bow on a bow = more potential energy with the same amount of space= more force.   I know that might be hard to follow since I am not using any technical terms, but hopefully that will explain why recurve bows are so nice.

The reason for the recurve is to eliminate some vibration in the bow and turn horizontal energy into vertical energy. The recurve shape means that the ends of the limbs are accelerating faster than the rest of the limb therefore you get more arrow speed. Also in terms of stored energy and energy efficiency (not regarding bow shape) whichever bow has the least weight (physical weight of the bow) will usually have the most efficient energy transfer. If your target isn't locked down solid, you will always have an unseen variable in your test for depth. The movement of the target as it's being struck will cause the arrow's penetrating force to lessen. Try setting a bale of hay on top of your target to weigh it down so it wont move. or just find some way to stabilize it.

I've read a lot of comments that try to explain why the recurve is shooting more efficiently, yet none thus far has mentioned the most important aspect: mass.   When you loose an arrow, you're not only accelerating the arrow, you're also accelerating the bow itself. Heavier limbs and tips mean less energy is going towards the arrow, thus a slower shooting bow.   the "subscriber" bow is made out of 3/4 inch PVC and it DOES NOT contain fiberglass rods, it's simply heated and tapered, which means it's thicker at the handle and gets flatter towards the tips. This results in less overall mass. The recurve design essentially has the effect of having a longer limbed bow, making it more efficient. It also reduces an effect called "stack" (meaning that the draw will feel more even rather than get harder to pull towards the end as the angle between the tip and the string reach 90 degrees, as is the case in longbows).   The reason why the "monster bow" didn't perform as well is mainly because you weren't pulling it all the way back, longbows have a tendency to stack towards the end of the draw, meaning that you're losing a lot of energy by not drawing it all the way, even if it's 2 inches or so less than full-draw. Another reason is that it has significantly more mass including that of the larger 1-inch pipe and the fiberglass rods.  One might think, "wait, aren't the fiberglass rods and larger diameter pipe supposed to have more power?" the answer is yes, they do, however, it's an issue of balancing that extra power with the extra mass.   Put simply, light and well designed bow of decent or even light draw weight is a better performer than bulky, more massive longbow of very heavy draw weight.

I know this is an older video, but wanted to leave a comment. You are correct about the recurve shape being superior to the regular long bow despite the draw weight difference. But if you would like to get more out of the long bow, on reason that your arrow did not penetrate deeper  could be either the arrow weight or spine. If the bow is pulling at 70, you should be shooting a 340 spine at a minimum of 350 grains of total weight. Anything less than that and you loose Kinetic Energy through arrow flexion. over longer distance, the wobble (archer's paradox) will stop and flatten out, but until it does you are loosing KE.   Kinetic Energy in archery is measured by:   KE = (arrow speed at distance)^2 (arrow weight in grains) / 450,240  if you can get a chronometer and get an average speed of the bow, you can tinker with various arrow weights. It is super fascinating stuff.

the recurve accellerates faster. since the arrow is so light, both bows will be able to accelerate it to the maximum speed of the limbs. a heavier arrow will travel more slowly in the lower weight bow and at a certain point the inability of the recurve bow to overcome inertia will result in speeds lower than the 75lbr even though the stronger bow is slower during dry fire. good bowhunters tailor thier bow/ arrows weight and use different combos for different game/ranges.  if making your own bow, something is better than nothing but certain designs will always out preform others. crossbows>compound>recurve>flatbow>longbow.  materials used also have different properties. some wood may have good compression properties and make strong bows but may not be a fast to return as others. laminating is easy with a table saw and yeilds good results.  or just buy a 100$ chinese bow off amazon. i can attest that they can be quite good.

I guess when u drawback a recurve the string of the bow is getting longer, which makes it works as if it was a longer bow. And when released the string gets shorter as the arrow is accelerating and the energy transfer to the now shorter string makes the string straighten out faster than what it would be. So the trick here is to pull longer to store the same amount of energy as a heavier bow.
Wow man that is good ilike this
Different bow styles accelerate the arrow in different ways.  The recurve bow fires an arrow with the evenest distribution of velocity, longbow fires with most of the acceleration first and the least when the arrow is released, and the compound bow or pully bow fires an arrow with the least at full draw and most at arrow release. All of it is a preference to the archer or situation, each affecting range, accuracy, ballistics etc.

These bows work better as a fibreglass single rod and then only using the PVC for the grip about 1/3rd the total length. You can get fibreglass electric fencing rods and small wire clamps that attach to them for the ends to attach the string.  Full length PVC blows up after its a few years old and exposed to some sun, becomes very brittle. The flying shards are dangerous to eyes, I got the fastest and safest bows from fibreglass full length bow with a 1/3rd middle PVC grip.

Impressive
My first PVC bow-making efforts involved stuffing the PVC full of fiberglass rods; it increased the draw weight tremendously, but decreased the power of the bow.  Eventually I realized that increased draw weight doesn't help unless it increases acceleration of the arrow, so let's look at what interferes with acceleration.  The bow I built had two major defects: all the fiberglass rods led to internal friction and movement -- wasted energy -- and the sheer mass of the fiberglass towards the ends of the bow worked against acceleration.  (acceleration = force / mass, so higher mass means lower acceleration; and the worst place to put that mass is the part of the bow that has to move the fastest.)  On top of that, the maximum acceleration of the bow is limited by whichever material returns to its original shape more slowly, PVC or fiberglass; it becomes a worst of both worlds situation.  So it makes sense to me that your bow with the heaviest draw weight is not providing the penetration you were expecting: sure you're putting a lot more energy into drawing the bow, but the bow isn't giving that energy back efficiently.

Well I wasnt able to read all coments, but the simple explanation is  that a recurve saves more  "power" per pull-lenght, because its a not linear spring. That means endpullforce doesen't matter. If you have a small up, in 1/2 way pull you have a bit more force in the middel-pull lenght, lets say 3/4 of endpullforce. But at 100% Pull lengt you have 4/4 pull force.  Its the  amount of stored energy per pull, that is higher on a recurve designe. Just simpel mechanics.

A simple bow works by rapidly releasing the stored energy, due to tension, in an elastically stressed curve when the drawn string is released. A recurve bow has three curves under tension when drawn, each of which releases the elastic deformation upon it faster than a bow made of the same material with only one curve. Therefore while the amount of stored energy is the same in both a single curved and recurve bow of the same draw weight, the recurve bow is able to release that energy faster and hence increase the ballistic performance of the test arrow.

So the shape of the arc has everything to do how the arrow will leave the string.

Cool bows
A recurve bow translates the draw weight more efficiently as it imparts its energy over the full period of the release. It is still accelerating the arrow when the longbow is starting to decelerate as it straightens out. It does not initially accelerate the arrow as fast as a longbow but releases more of its energy into the arrow when it is already in motion. Basically the same HP,  but the recurve has a second gear.

Suggestion on how to draw a bow and not wear your arm out and get max draw easily on the more powerful bow. Bring the bow above your head and hold the line with the arrow near your face and then as you bring the bow to level for firing, you stretch your arm out so you are pushing the bow away from you. Less fatigue means more times to fire the bows and the max power from the bows you use.

Hungarians and Magyars used recurve/composite bows for a reason. They work extremely well, are shorter for horseback, easier to use (lower draw for comparable power to longer bows), but they certainly were not cheap back in the day, they were considered state of the art and sometimes took months to make thanks to glues and other supplies like horn. Look , maybe I'm not an expert , but to me a recurve bow, it's like a 1 1/2 bow in one, meaning that , the adding of an additional curve to both end tips of the main bow , adds more power to the draw, in other words the ''little'' ''bows'' at the tips , bend first , then it's the turn of the ''main' bow, so to speak , at release you'll get ,first,  the little ones , then the main one , the adding forces are of course greater in the end. Et voila !!  a ''recurve bow !

The monster bow is considered a long bow Wich shoots closer but if you shoot farther away then you have to rainbow shoot it with decreases the power but with recurves they are made for both long and close shots
The recurve bow gets closer to full draw-weight at smaller draw-lengths and then evens out toward the end of the draw. That means that a high draw weight is applied to the arrow for longer. If you remember F=ma (force equals mass times acceleration), that means that the arrow is accelerating quickly for longer meaning that it will reach a higher speed. Just a heads-up, this is about to get kind of complicated. If you like pictures, check out this slideshow, and then come back if you want my explanation:https://www.slideshare.net/ManoharMHegde/design-of-limb-of-a-takedown-recurve-bow  For more practical analysis, kinetic engery (KEâ‰ˆ1/2mvÂ²  Kinetic energy approximately equals half the mass times the square of the velocity) is usually used. And the energy of a full draw is measured PE=F*d (Potential energy equals force times distance.)  You might have noticed I said PE instead of KE: I'll come back to that. Basically, you could come up with the "Draw Energy"  of the bow (I just made that up) by pulling the string back a half-inch, or an inch at a time and measuring the force at each distance. Then, you would multiply each force with the distance it was measured at, and add up the products. (The smaller increments you use, the more accurate your measurement will be, it just depends on how much time you want to spend.) When you let go of the string, you release the energy in the bow, and it converts to kinetic energy following the rules I mentioned at the beginning (that's why, for the string, we measure PE).

To my understanding, the recurve is a way of shortening the overall spacial length of the bow while maintaining the power of a long bow. It's like the bow is longer without actually being longer. Why would you want a shorter bow? For one, it makes horseback archery significantly easier, but on a more practical level, it gives you better mobility in the tightness of dense forests, lessening the likelihood of snagging your bow and making noise that would scare your prey.

Nice video, however that's not how you test the draw weight of your bow. First you need to know the draw distance for each one of them 24 inch, 26, etc. Knowing that, you need a tiller, which is very easy to make, look it up, you put the bow on it, and draw it to  draw distance with a bow scale, a device that measures the weight directly. The  number displayed by the scale is the draw weight.  You're correct, the recurves on a bow add more poundage for a set bow length, like you said, the end of the bow snap back further, increasing arrow speed. Is a trademark of horse archers, being  on a horse you can't wield a huge bow, the back of the horse and the saddle get in the way, so by having recurves on your bow, you get a more powerful one in a smaller dimension.
The longbow pull stacks up -- string pressure falls off rapidly after the string is released.  The recurve has a flatter pull-pressure curve and so continues to accellerate the arrow longer -- as different parts of the bow bend at different pressures.
The reason why the recurve bow is more powerful is because if it did not have the curves at the ends it of course would be a regular bow but the bow is already strong and powerful but since the curves are there it adds stopping power witch is about an extra fifteen pounds of pleasure but since it dose have those curves at the end the bow strings tighten up making it mor powerful

Regarding your penetration test: Aside from the mechanical advantage of the recurve bow, it looks like you have a bow tuning issue. As you see in your video, the arrow flexes as it comes out of the bow, and also came out at an angle. Arrows always flex. But if properly tuned, they should come out flexing left and right, and horizontally straight.  Anyway, if an arrow hits the target while flying slightly sideways, penetration is massively reduced.

It's not a mystery about the heavier bow by adding so much more material you have effectively reduced the flexibility of the bow while you made it stronger you also slowed its reaction time down which means your arrows don't fly as fast
It's not about the maximum force, it's actually a function of the force (pounds) times the time it's at that force. For the monster bow, the force increases at about the same rate relative to how far you pull it, but with the recurve bow, it reaches the full force much earlier and keeps it much farther into the draw. So even though the MAXIMUM value of force will be the same, the area under the force curve(the integral of the force function) is going to lead to a higher final velocity of the arrow.   If this still doesn't help, think about being in your car. Your monster bow will slowly push the pedal Down farther and farther all the way to the floor for 10 seconds. But your recurve bow stomps on the gas right from the beginning  Hope this helps!

I'm late to the game, but I think there are two things giving the recurve the same velocity as the high powered longbow.  1.  Because the recurve has a lower draw weight, you are able to pull it back farther.  So upon release, the string applies force to the arrow for a greater distance, thus accelerating the arrow for longer than the longbow does.  2.  The shape of the recurve means that when loaded, more of the tension on the string is directed along the flight of the arrow.  This results in the recurve having a larger percentage of the total draw weight being applied in the direction of the arrows flight, as compared to the longbow.

Recurves bows attenuates vibrations when you release and the arrow "leave" the rope. So arrow ends on target with less "shaking" path, more precisely and penetrating. You should observe this with target on different distances. In near distance a a long bow could be less penetrating when in long distance you should not observe significant differences... Hey, fellow bowyer here, though I work with wood. I thought I would give you an answer to your question about what the purpose is for the recurve; it's kind of two fold. First off, it shortens the bow. This means that it can store more energy per pound/per inch. Second, having those ears (the top quarter or so of the bow limb) bent away from you and towards the target makes the bow want to launch its energy much more forcefully. This, in effect, "flattens" out the cast so that there is less arching in the arrow's flight.   The reason your penetration test had some skewed results, in my opinion, is the result of you using the same arrow for several bows all of differing draw weight. An arrow spined for a 35 lb bow is going to behave much differently than a bow of 75 lbs. A better test would be to test arrows spined to the bow you are using but each weighing the same amount. If you buy your arrows, their weight is measured in "grains". There are ways to measure the weight of homemade arrows though. I did not see this in the other comments unless I missed it... Another advantage of recurve bows is that a lot of the bow arm mass is moving more up and down and so it cancels out.  You always have the reciprocal momentum of launching the arrow but with a longbow you also have the momentum of the bow arms being accelerated forward and then stopping which does not cancel out.  Of course, the recurve bow arms do have some forward momentum too but not as much.

Recurves, horn and Mongolian bows are able to seriously stack power at lighter weights and extreme draw lengths. Jim Hamm and several articles in the Traditional Bowyers Bibles series do the math and its unbelievable the lost secrets of 5000 years of the bent stick. Medieval yew war bows pulling 100-130lbs @28" were mass produced and so were their heavy grain arrows. They along with crossbow bolts were heavily grained for armour (yeah I know it sounds British but that's how I spell it) piercing. Regardless, Turkish bows still carry all world distance records. My fathers friends brought Laotian, Vietnamese and Cambodian crossbows back from the war from 1950-'75 and these had longer bamboo arrows. I made a modified version of your 5ft longbow. No idea about the pull strength, but I made mine 6ft with 10 driveway markers, and it gets 11" penetration on a hollow wooden door. That being said, I cut 4 of the 48" markers in half and staggered the lengths, so... short/long matches up with long/short inside the PVC shaft in order to stagger the support (hopefully) without creating weak points. I also added a grip using a bandana and some hose clamps. Thank you so much for the video, though; I wouldn't have been inspired to build this thing if not for you!

So, I don't know if someone commented yet, but what you experienced with your recurve is what drew me in to shooting recurve bows. When I was deciding between longbows and recurves, everything I researched said that the strings on recurves stay in contact with the arrow for a longer period of time, resulting in more time for acceleration and more force behind each shot. Compounds stop way short of both long and recurve, and long bows still stop quite a bit shorter than recurves. The shape of the limbs just provide that extra push, and you get quite a bit more power  for less effort. When choosing arrows or arrow materials, you kinda have to take the extra force in to consideration as well. Er, that's if you shoot wooden or fiberglass. Wonderful world of bows.

what you have actually found is the spring limit of pvc + (internal fibreglass rod), ie although it takes 70 lb to curve the bow, the spring speed which pvc will return to natural shape has a limit. this means that your 35 and 45 lb bows are at peak spring rate for the material. return speed + weight = arrow speed.  to increase arrow speed you must increase bow return speed and apparently pvc speed is at maximum at approx 45lb. increasing the internal fibreglass rod weight may help, BUT, the natural speed of pvc will expenetially keep the speed to a lower limit.  look at it like this...... a car can do a top speed eg 100......and  a sports car will do eg 150. if the sport car were to tow the slower car, neither car will reach 150 even if both cars are at full throttle.  the slow car is only making power to 100 and after that speed it is dead weight to the sports car between 100 and 150. the top speed during towing will be eg 125. if you increase the top speed of the sport car on its own by 20, when towing the slower car it wont tow at the full 20 faster, maybe 10 mph increase. the pvc is the slow car and the fibreglass rods are the sport car.   hope this analogy helps.

Another thing that will help increase draw weight is the cord length. The shorter the length, the more tension on the bow and therefore, a heavier draw weight
there's a book out there called the bowyers bible. one of the things it talks about is the relationship between string angle and draw weight. lowering the string angle (usually by reflexing the tips) lowers the draw weight while still delivering the same amount of energy to the arrow. another way of looking at that is that a higher draw weight doesn't necessarily mean a more powerful bow. what determines a bows power is how fast it shoots an arrow of a given weight. I bet if you chronographed all those bows you would find out that the reflexed bow was shooting faster. I believe you can still legally download the bowyers bible volume one online for free
The recurve is naturally more powerful than the long if both are made with the same material. It's going to shoot more springy. I've always been curious as to how a low-elastic rubber would do as a back on a PVC bow...

just looking at the comparison: the monster bow, when you draw it back, looks to be about 22" from hand to hand. the recurve bow 'pushes' your holding-hand farther forward and at the same time curls back on the ends, giving you about 28-30" from hand to hand. the 'recurve' seems to 'spring forward' upon release. this may explain why the monster bow and recurve shoot about the same speed....just the way it looked to me when i viewed it.

Nice video. The recurve on a bow does assist a lot with kicking more power for sure. Which is why you get about the same penetration with the lower poundage recurve in relation to the higher poundage traditional style. The recurve bow is able to store more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an equivalent straight-limbed bow. Which in turn gives a greater amount of energy and speed to the arrow. However personally I do prefer compound bows over recurves for the simple fact they have the let-off when at full draw. Which makes the bow easier to fire as you're usually only holding around 30% of the draw weight with a compound when at full draw.

I do know that a recurve bow is longer in length,  increases the draw length and power stroke from the string to the riser. 15 to 20 percent typically. It reduces torque, which is more forgiving to a shooters form. There is also more mass forward rotation of the limbs, rather than outward, so more energy is transferred forward to the arrow instead of outward from the string. Therefore your lighter recurve is as powerful as your longbow because it is more efficient.  Your longbows major flaw is that it uses more material rather than stiffer material of equal mass. more mass means more energy absorption into itself, and friction as the glass rods rub against each other. Assuming you were pulling the 75 pound bow back all the way, most likely the reason the penetration was so bad was because of the mass of the limbs. Outer limbs on bows are narrowed so that the extra mass does not take away from the recoil speed of the limbs. Essentially you had the functional equivalent of a low rpm/high torque motor. You had more energy but you needed a much heavier arrow to take advantage of it. If you were to retest using a heavier arrow, assuming the bow is decent, it will penetrate considerably deeper than the lighter arrow.
Recurves apparently came from a place where trees suitable for long bows didn't grow. Being shorter, they were more able to be shot in any direction from horseback.Saw it on the history channel

The arrow is sort of bottlenecking your performance.   A bow with higher draw weight takes a heavier and stiffer arrow, because otherwise much of the force is lost due to the amount of "flex" in the arrow. I think you'd find if you stuck that same arrow in a 100lb draw weight bow, you wouldn't actually be able to get more penetration. (Even if you could pull it.) You might even break the shaft of the arrow.   You could try the same thing again with a stiffer, heavier arrow, and you should get a more noticeable difference between the two.   The only other possibility I can imagine would be internal friction in the longbow. Perhaps the rods shift against one another during draw and release?   Generally a recurve just allows you a longer draw in a smaller bow. You get those little Mongol archery recurve bows which are quite small, but have quite high draw weight and able to take a full-length draw. You couldn't do that with a short "self" straight bow, or you'd have to make it so whip-tailed that it wouldn't nearly as hard as its draw weight might have you believe, and would be prone to failure.

+ZNA ProductionsÂ Just because a bow has a heavy draw weight does not make the limbs snap forwardÂ quicker (and propel the arrows quicker). The shape of the recurve is what makes the arrows quicker off the string - if you follow the path that tip of the limbs take during the draw and release cycle, the path drawn by the tips of the recurve bow is flatterÂ than that of the path drawn by the longbow, resulting in a quicker acceleration of the arrow, and thereby more efficient delivery of the projectile. Also, that last 2 or 3 inches is critical for getting toÂ the total draw weight of the bow. My recurveÂ jumps from 40ish poundsÂ to 55# over the last 3 inches of my draw cycle; this is because draw weight increases exponentially with increased draw length (for the effective usefulÂ range of the bow). Basically, the limbs of the longbow squish together more when you pull it back and they don'tÂ get the arrow going as fastÂ as quickly as the recurve bow; so when you're going traditional, aim (#puns) for limb quickness as a priority over draw weight.

Recurve Bow isÂ  preloaded with limb tension there fore every inch drawn back stores more energy more evenlyÂ though the full draw cycle.. Your longbow version will take 2 or 3 inches of pull back before limbs start storing usefulÂ energy and as you have trouble drawing bow to full draw you are losing more draw length distance andÂ energy.Â Â Result similar power output. If longbow could be pulled back by theÂ  hulk 100% it should be better. But as an archer accuracyÂ  is more desirable to power for kill shots if bow too hard to drawÂ then accuracyÂ  suffers too.

The recurve bow has a more efficient draw. The shape and cross section of the limbs allow for more energy storage, and a shorter distance to accelerate over than a longbow. Longbow limbs need to move much further (takes more time) to move an arrow the equal distance. The result is a less accelerated arrow than one fired from a recurve bow.

Ill explain the difference of the recurve bow from you're heavier long bow style. Recurve bows are designed to launch an arrow at a fast and powerful speed with a lower draw weight making it easier to use.Longbows are the superior of the two but requires a much higher skill base to use it properly. Because they require a higher level of training and take more time to effectively use them recurves became more popular with many cultures. The perfect example of this would be the English longbow who were renowned and feared because of the power and effectiveness the bows were, but the reason the french didn't use longbows was because of the skill requirement and the time it took to train the archers.

Sweet, sweet video.  I'm stoked about this bow design!   Yes, the recurve design is more efficient in terms of arrow energy vs. full draw weight.  The energy stored in the bow is a function of it's draw weight at EVERY point in the draw.  The recurve has what's referred to as a "plump" draw curve, meaning the draw weight goes up sooner in the draw, storing more energy for a given full draw weight.  The "Bowyer's Bible" series goes into this in detail.  It's easier to understand once you see some draw weight vs draw length graphs.

:O  A recurve bow is a bow with limbs that curve away from the archer when unstrung. ... Recurved limbs also put greater strain on the materials used to make the bow, and they may make more noise with the shot. Really well made stuff, cool.

The power of a bow isn't only affected by the maximum force needed to pull the string back, but also the force needed to pull the first bit. The recurved bow does a great deal of pull-back in the beginning, while the simple bow is extremely easy to pull back the first bit even if it's really stiff.   The recurved bow is basically a shorter bow before you pull it back, and it becomes longer and lower, so that it gets easier (or almost the same) to pull back the longer back you pull it.

The point of a normal recurve bow is to get additional power out of the same length of the same material.  Normal bows would use a straight length of wood, which you bend for power.  With a recurve bow you take an already curved bit of wood, *bend it in the opposite way to string it* (thus the re-curve part), and so you're actually bending it farther than the straight wood, since it's starting point is curved forward.  So, it'd be like this: *-( <- you holding the unstrung bow *D <- you holding the strung bow, it hasn't rotated, it's bent backward.  Not sure if your subscriber bow was shaped already re-curved, or if it's genuine.  Either way, power is still based on draw weight, you just get more draw weight out of the same length of the same material.

Draw weight is dependent on draw length and draw length is dependent on the shooters height. For instance I am 5' 10'' and my bow is 65lbs, at 28.5 in. draw. Also what you made is not a long bow. A long bow is a bow that is longer than the shooter is tall when it is unstrung.
what ware those yellow sticks
Recurves have far more preloaded energy in the limbs than longbows. So, a recurve can have 20 lbs less draw weight but more stored energy in the shape of the limbs than the longbow with a higher draw weight and less stored energy. This explains why you got equal penetration from both bows. Compound bows work similarly with the construction of their limb and cam systems. There are 50-60 lb bows that outshoot 70 lb bows in the speed department just because they are made to be more efficient.
To answer your question about recurve vs longbow or D bow as it's also known, a D bow will have a straight linear power curve from 0 to 30 let's say, whereas a recurve will have a bit of a taper near the top of the power curve so it gets a little easier to draw back as you reach full draw with it. In essence, you're right, it is more efficient. Conversely, a modern compound bow will feel very hard to draw at first, but it gets much easier as you draw back. At full draw on a compound, you might only be holding around 10 lbs, even though the bow itself is rated for 100 lbs on release. Very interesting project :)

I haven't checked if anyone has already pointed it out, but I'd like to inform on the subject of recurved bows. The Turks, Huns and the Mongolians used them throughout the history. It is nearly as powerful as twice as an uncurved bow of the same lenght and material (which would not really apply to the one you made, since the authentic ones have a much deeper curve, but the same physics apply to the lenght of the curve you made). All the civilisations I counted used to be mostly on horseback, so they needed much smaller bows. That's why they developped this thecnique in bow crafting. It did nearly the same amount of damage (in masterpieces even more), while being small and practical.  I don't know how to explain why the curve leads to more tension scientifically, so I'll use everyday language. Think of the tension of a normal bow when you draw it. If you pay attention, the curved bow as three tension points: on each highest point of the curves on the corners, and in the middle. Those little ones make up for nearly another middle tension, making it twice as powerful as a normal bow.   I hope its been helpful to you, and to anyone who watched the video that was curious about the answer. Good day to everyone.

Work (Energy) = Force x Distance  So even if the draw strength is the same, if one bow has longer draw than the other it will likely be stronger (higher total energy to arrow).  Also traditionally recurve bows's natural curve is opposite that of the stringed configuration. This means that it is already severely deformed in zero-draw, and hence has higher draw strength in general. This allows for smaller, more compact bow of same draw strength as say straighter long bow. I don't know if draw length is longer though, intuition tells me that it would be shorter if anything.

recurve where made popular in ancient times because they where able to achieve more velocity in a shorter bow (recurve) long bows where very large (long) in order to achieve velocity making them very unwieldy on horse back, hence why a recurve bow is less forgiving than a long bow. long bows were not as durable and more difficult to carry long distances into battle due to their size/length. the draw was also shorter on a recurve with higher pull weight compared to a long bow if that makes sense. but in the end, the mechanical compound bow is the pinnacle of bow technology using pulleys to make the draw technically long with less pull weight at full draw giving you the forgiveness of a long bow, the power and compact size of a recurve with the steroid velocity of compound physics and a set draw so once you achieve the draw weight pull all the way back, the pulleys allow it to rest with minimal resistance until fired and shabang.

I shoot flat bows as a hobby and this is what i know about draw weight and efficiency.   Obviously an arrow shot from a flat, long, or recurve bow flexes a lot while it's being driven by the cord. It's essential for accurate flight and optimal power for it to do so. Most English warbows drew at around 75 to 85 lbs but could exceed 100 in extreme cases. To utilize this correctly they used arrows that were much heavier and stiffer, often times consisting of two types of wood "footed".   To sum up, the likely reason your recurve hits about as hard with the same arrow as your 75lb long bow is due to the flex of the arrow shaft. You are likely losing a lot of momentum from the arrow over flexing.   PS the main advantage of recurve bows is packing more draw weight into a shorter bow. Useful if one primarily shoots from horseback.

the recurve bow is originally designed to be a bow for low level archers, like for student archers that couldn't yet get a full draw back on a longbow. It has more range than a standard  bow, but its range is just short of the longbow range. Basically, just a bow for archers without the training and strength in the back muscles of a professional one.

haven't read all the comments yet I'm sure you've already got one that helps you understand the recurve bow. The idea of the recurve bow is that when it is at its most relaxed the Moe has more leverage on the line because it is a shorter distance between the two points where the line makes contact with the boat. As you draw it back the line let's go of some of the bow and you get more leverage from the line against the bus because the contact points are farther from Center. it is a similar effect to a compound bow in that as you fully extend it it becomes easier to draw and as it comes back to being relaxed and actually increases in strength. Granted the compound bow has much more drastic of an effect then recurve

woah IDFWU
Here is a little of the math behind arrow penetration power.  The depth an arrow penetrates is related to the kinetic energy behind it.  There are several formulas for kinetic energy, but the easiest one to apply here is the formula for the work done drawing the arrow. Newton's second law tells us that Work=-Potential Energy (PE), and that the KE of the arrow after the string stops acting on it is equal to the potential energy it had when it was released. W = -PE = KE = F*s (where s is distance).  Judging from a quick little mockup in Paint (http://prnt.sc/bntdov), your draw distance is maybe an inch or two shorter with the 70 lb bow, but that doesn't account for the decreased depth of penetration given that the draw weight is just over 50% higher in the long bow.   So here is the missing link: You said that you tested the bow's draw weights, but that only accounts of it's force at full draw (or in the case of a compound bow, right before the cams let off the hold weight), but the bows really have a draw weight profile.  At 1" of draw, it might only be 3 lbs, at 2", it may be 6 lbs, and so on all the way up to the final draw weight at full draw.  You would actually have to apply the calculus version of the KE=F*s equation of (http://prnt.sc/bntflp).  Basically, the force at each point along the draw is not a constant 45 or 70 lbs.    If you don't feel like trying to gather a collection of points and then fit a formula to that collection of points, and then integrate that function for the draw length, you would probably find it easier to measure the force at one inch intervals from the neutral position to the full draw position. Then, because you used 1 inch intervals, you could add up the draw weights at each interval and this would give you a number that could be used to compare the bow's strengths. It won't be the arrow's kinetic energy after release, but it will be scaled equally in such a way that it would allow you to compare their respective power.  Now, if I remember a little about the purpose of the design of recurve bows, the shape allows for a more constant force profile across the draw weight where as a long bow changes more from start to finish.  Source: I'm a mechanical engineer with an interest in all forms of archaic weapons.

cool video. Draw weight is not a proper indicator of final arrow speed and terminal impact. i think it was in The Boyer's Bible volume 1, one of the authors tested a series of bows(like over 10 for sure) and the heaviest were among the poorest performers for final distance an arrow would fly from a 45 degree launch. this is likely why your 1 inch thick bow doesn't get better penetration in spite of having more rods and way higher draw weight. its more a question of how fast a bow string returns to zero on a given bow versus another. also the recurve design has thinner limbs returning to 0 far more quickly and efficiently overall compared to the other(untillered longbow?) design. the limbs on it are practically getting in the way of a return to 0. don't gett me wrong though . love this design for simplicity alone and would totally make one!!!

So you like bows and didn't know that the recurved bow from the Gemgis Kan were a lot deadlier than the great British bow? It allowed enough dexterity to shoot while riding a pony with the same power although smaller and with less strength needed.
Force times the distance you can pull back gives the kinetic energy you're imparting on to the arrow. Since you can't pull the monster bow back as far, the bow imparts force on the arrow for a shorter distance. That's why the smaller draw weight but longer pull distance is able to get as high speeds as the higher draw weight and short pull distance. Equation: kinetic energy = 1/2mv^2 = force times distance. Of course the force is not constant when the string is released. But you get the idea.

Poundage vs limb weight is important with bows. You can have a 100 lb bow, but if the limbs are heavy and not aerodynamic, you won't get as fast of a velocity on the arrows. The recurve bows stores more energy in a more compact and lighter package, so the velocity is more efficiently transferred.

in case you never got an answer about why you get more force from the recurve bow with a less substantial draw weight.  the force of a bow depends on the draw weight and the draw distance. a greater draw distance means more force from the bow snapping back into place and more time the arrow is being directly pushed by the string. a recurve bow essentially starts off with more pressure on the string, trying to snap back. on top of that the fact that it tends to have a shorter distance between the string and the center of the bow allows for a more substantial draw distance. that's why

Hi ZNA, the purpose of the recurves is the same as the purpose of whip tillering, which is to accelerate the string more rapidly. So here's my guess: your massive bow is hard to bend, but it doesn't straighten out very fast so the energy is wasted. You might have a velocity problem because the thickness of the PVC is decelerating the tips of the bow, or you might have an issue because the shaping means that the tips can't decompress fast enough because they're encountering opposing compressive force from the other side of the bow and their peak velocity is too slow and in the wrong place relative to shot timing. Around 45lb draw is where you start seeing innovations including whip tillering - making the ends of the bow much thinner so they straighten in a whip-like motion - or recurving as well as composite construction (horn and sinew, or yew which is a 'natural composite' and always used this way in bowmaking) and far heavier arrows. Very light arrows are probably off the string of a slow-moving heavy bow before they have been fully 'loaded' with the straightening force of the bow - give it a punt with a hard-spined overweight arrow and see what you get. My guess is the performance of the low weight bows nosedives, the midweight 45lb one falls slightly and the 75lb one becomes more effective. Cool bow by the way.

Recurve bows were developed for light cavalry. Some of the most famous being the Scythians and Parthians Called the Parthian shot and later called the parting shot. The shorter bow was more useful on horseback but had similar power due to the recurve and materials used to make it more stiff. Long bows of course were used by infantry, the most famous probably the English Longbow, which was so instrumental in the defeat of the French heavy cavalry at the Battle of Agincourt in 1415. A common practice by the French was to cut off the two fingers used by the English bowmen to draw their bows, this led to the British insult to the French of showing their two fingers in the sign later used to signify Victory by the British. The Iranians and others would cut off the hand of an enemy warrior before release so he could no longer wield a weapon against them, in ancient times. War is indeed hell.

REASON RE-CURVE IS BETTER       If you examine the firing of the recurve bow and the large bow, you can see how the string acts upon release. On the big bow, the body is straight and so the string returns to original position and stops. However, the recurve bow is naturally bent and these joints hyper-extend upon release, allowing the string to carry beyond original position, thus lengthening the "launch distance," for lack of a better term. It works based on a few principles of physics, but basically to make something go fast you need either A: A very strong force, or B: a long distance of transfer. When the big bow energy is transferred, the draw strength allows lots of energy, but some is lost naturally. By allowing the arms and string to hyper-extend, you increase the distance over which the energy transfers, allowing more time for more energy. Energy=power  Layman's terms: The longer the arrow is on the string the high the speed it will have.

By the way, the knot I used for my loops is a very easy, minimalist one that's easily adjustable called the "Archery Loop." Look for it in a knot book by Geoffrey Budworth. To compute the 'power' measure the force required to pull the bow each increment of distance such as every centimeter and add up the product of the force times the increment. Mixing units: if it takes 1 lb to pull the first cm that is 1 lb-cm of energy, if it takes 2 lb to get the next centimeter then add 2lb-cm to the 1lb-cm for 3lb-cm. Do so until you have pulled the bow as far as you are going to when shooting.  Do the same for each bow and the one for which that sum is the greatest will have the greatest power.  Force*distance= work, work is energy.   The recurve changes the amount of force needed for each particular increment of pulling. The force required to bend the pipe increases as the pipe is bent more, the recurve is bent more to start with so the force is higher during the whole span of the string being pulled.
I left work and went to The Home Depot and rounded up my supplies; then I went home and before I even had anything to eat, I set about making this project.  I don't have any arrows yet for testing it, but it seems to have been a complete success!  I want to mention that once I was bending the bow to attach the string, I found that the bow didn't bend true to the notches I had made; the natural arch that it wanted to make set the notches off-center. So, to remedy that, I made two new notches, each about a half inch toward the center from the original two. So I recommend making the notches as the last step after inserting the fiberglass rods.  Also: Rather than using 550 paracord, I used 425 paracord, which I believe is better suited to this task because instead of an oval cross-section, it has a round cross-section.  Thanks again for the tutorial!

Its a bit late, but recurve bows shoot the arrow faster, but with less force. This means that with a lighter arrow, it will do better than non-recurves, but it will not be able to launch heavier arrows as fast as stronger, non-recurve bows will.
Each curve in a recurve bow stores energy as the bow is being drawn, and does so without requiring much additional strength from the user. Instead of a single curve releasing its stored energy when the arrow is released, you have 3 curves simultaneously releasing their energy, much like an arm throwing a ball. Each joint in the arm propels the ball forward as it straightens out, your wrist being the last of which to generate force. Recurve bows have always amazed me, just because of the fact that they have been around for centuries. LONG before anyone had a working knowledge of physics.

The bowstring rides around the curve on a recurve bow. This takes up the string much faster than a longbow. F=MA, which mean that the faster of acceleration (A) placed upon the mass of the arrow (M), the more force (F) it has when it hits. A lighter pound  bow that can accelerate an arrow faster than a much heavier pull bow will impart more force to the arrow. Too many people get hung up on draw weight. A bow has to accelerate its own mass as well as the mass of the arrow. Too much mass in the bow will actually slow it down.

I can try to explain the diference in power between the rather mongolian pattern recurve and your pvc bow, but I warn you, im not good at explaining mechanics  In a nutshell, It is flexibility. If you watch this video, if you look at the recurve you will see how the whole bow bends as you draw, and how little the pvc does. That flexibility gives the recurve a longer draw than a longbow, as well as adding  the momentum of the frame snapping back into place and Bingo bango bongo, more power, less bow.

As an archer the recurve bow that has a fifty pound draw shoots as far as a single curve (or simple) bow with a seventy five pound draw. The extra curves are like a whipping effect on the arrow it leaves at the normal speed then when it finishes the launch I whips it forward
Just because a bow is heavier to draw doesn't mean it will be faster.  The PVC pipe is extremely inefficient.  Why do you think limbs are supposed to taper to the tip?  Less weight at the tips means they will travel faster.  Saying you made a "POWERFUL" bow is just a lie.  You made an inefficient bow that will take lots of set and quickly start shooting slower and slower as it takes more and more set over time.  I'd recommend that nobody wastes their time making something like this.  Use hardwood and research how to make a real, long-lasting, efficient selfbow.

hey bro cool video. you mentioned why both the monster bow and the other recurved bow had the same penetrating power even though the recurved is less poundage. the recurved bow has three points of contact that bend and snap back, where as the monster bow has one point. the three points of contact are all snapping back simultaneously increasing the speed of the bow snap back = faster arrow movement.

the recurve works by shortening the string (the part that is free and not touching the limbs) so that the angle at the nock of the arrow stays more acute (less flat, like when you are at the end of the drawing) and the energy transfer is more efficient. It's like shortening the limbs, at the same draw weight, while the arrow gets released.

a bow is a third class lever because when you pull in the middle thatâ€™s where the effort is and the fulcrum is on the right. The load is in the middle so a bow can be a second-class lever too. static equilibrium of levers fe*de=fl*dl ; force effort times the distance effort is equal to the force load times the distance load.  which means when you change the distance effort the force load is going to be greater or less. your re-curve bow is just a better lever.

Just a quick wiki to your questions about the recurve's purpose in a bow, and why it makes the effect you saw in this video. Thanks for this toturial, very cool :)  A recurve bow is a bow  with limbs that curve away from the archer when unstrung. A recurve bow  stores more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an  equivalent straight-limbed bow, giving a greater amount of energy and  speed to the arrow. A recurve will permit a shorter bow than the simple  straight limb bow for a given arrow energy and this form was often  preferred by archers in environments where long weapons could be  cumbersome, such as in brush and forest terrain, or while on horseback.

I found this on Wikipedia   A recurve bow is a bow with limbs that curve away from the archer when unstrung. A recurve bow stores more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an equivalent straight-limbed bow, giving a greater amount of energy and speed to the arrow. A recurve will permit a shorter bow than the simple straight limb bow for a given arrow energy and this form was often preferred by archers in environments where long weapons could be cumbersome, such as in brush and forest terrain, or while on horseback.

Something about that recurve may have to do with how it is structured. After a certain draw length, the potential energy from bending the bow had maxed out, further draw gives no more potential energy because the vector of the limbs are aligning with your draw's vector. Mean while the recurve bow may have a higher effective draw length than your monster bow, hence the result.

As far as how recurve bows work, they simply get more power out of a full draw from a shorter bow. A long bow of equal draw would be much larger but propel an arrow identically. I think you need to retest the draw of those bows, and possibly test your own draw limits.

A recurve bow shoots very flat, which means that at close range it will hit a target at approximately a 90 degree angle, for optimal penetration.  A Longbow has a significant arc when fired, which means that it will not have the same close range result as a recurve.   Longbows were historically fired at extreme range, which added a lot of armor piercing power.  Also, Longbows typically fired a bodkin arrow, which was very heavy and thereby was not affected as much by the arc of a loosed bow as a modern carbon arrow.  Hope this helps.

Great video, I learned some stuff! By the way, because the limbs of a recurve bow are bent forward, it takes more energy to draw the bow, which increases the draw weight. Recurve bows are more "Size efficient", meaning if you'll examin a long bow and recurve bow of the same size, the recuve will have higher draw weight. This advantage allows for making smaller bows with still high draw weight, but the penalty is that recurve bows wear out faster (Not 100% sure about that) and they are louder, as the string hits the limbs of the recurve bow.

Yes. Recurve bows allow the string to move at a much higher velocity than a longbows string, which will give equal power, with less draw weight. In fact, a 55lb pull recurve is about as powerful as a 90lb crossbow.

A lot of people don't realize how elastic paracord is... maybe the similarity of the penetration of the two bows has to do with draw power on the larger bow being lost through cord stretch. The penetration of the arrow is related to the distance you shoot. The arrow will start moving with a forward movement but also with a oscillation mouvement on the axis of the length of the arrow (due to compression forces of the pushing of the string). The oscillation subdues after a while (in miliseconds). When the oscillation disappears the arrow is at the peack of penetration capacity. Which means that an arrow needs some distance (40 feet should do) to reach its maximum penetration capacity. If you shooted the box from a longer distance your 70 pounds bow certainly  would have generated more penetration.

A recurve has the same draw weight over nearly the full draw, instead of the drasticly increasing draw weight of an longbow. Therefore the distance and time the bow has to accelerate the arrow ist much longer and the energy (given the same weight) put into the arrow is higher. adding that the bow itself is often way lighter the acceleration of the throwarms is way quicker and needs lesser power. Lastly the handshock is much weaker with a recurve but more vibrating.  Your arrow test is good for a basic Idea, but with the stronger bows, the arrow hits fairly sideways, wich reduces the impact depth. You would need to have an arrow with fitting spine for every draw weight (archers paradox). Moreover, the stronger bows wont throw much faster than the weak ones, but be able to keep a similar throwing speed with way heavier arrows, so that the arrow has more kinetic energy.

the amount of power contributed by the draw length increases exponentially the farther back you go. So if you are leaving like an inch in draw length on the 75lb bow you are leaving ALOT of extra power behind. Also, the recurve increases the draw length of the bow, so 2 bows of equal length, one recurve and one not will have different draw lengths and a disparity in power, with the recurve bow having the longer draw and greater power. The total weight of the bow, and in particular the weight at the tips of the bow will also affect power. So if you can find a way to shave mass off of the tips of a bow you can increase its strength as it will lose less energy just pushing the limbs forward on release, and send more power into the arrow itself.
the recurve bow has less draw with more power due to the fact that it's power comes at when it gets closer to its original position unlike other bows that's why you see elves using them in fantasy movies

One way a recurved bow gets its mysterious powers is from the snappiness it gives the arrow just before it leaves the bow. A normal bow have the most power when you release the string. And then gradually loose power until it is back to its normal position where the arrow leaves the bow. You can understand this if you ever pulled a bow. It is easy to draw in the beginning (low energy) and then becomes harder to draw the longer you draw (more energy is stored in the bow). It means the arrow is accelerating the most in the beginning and then the force from the string is gradually going away as the arrow is leaving the string.   The recurved bow works the same. But, just when the string is back to its normal position and the energy is low, and the arrow is about to leave the string the recurve effect kicks in. The recurve shape on the bow makes the string straighten out really fast and hard end (in its normal position). That gives the arrow a hard kick just as it is about to leave the string. The arrow has already gain most of its momentum from the beginning and its mass is already moving forward. This extra kick is really helping to increase the speed of the arrow.   There is a lot more to learn about the recurve bow and its other benefits. But hope this was somewhat helpful =)

from wikipedia: A recurve bow has tips that curve away from the archer when the bow is unstrung. By definition, the difference between recurve and other bows is that the string touches a section of the limb when the bow is strung. A recurve bow stores more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an equivalent straight-limbed bow, giving a greater amount of energy and speed to the arrow. A recurve will permit a shorter bow than the simple straight limb bow for a given arrow energy and this form was often preferred by archers in environments where long weapons could be cumbersome, such as in brush and forest terrain, or while on horseback. Recurved limbs also put greater strain on the materials used to make the bow, and they may make more noise with the shot. Extreme recurves make the bow unstable when being strung. An unstrung recurve bow can have a confusing shape and many Native American weapons, when separated from their original owners and cultures, were incorrectly strung backwards and destroyed when attempts were made to shoot them.[1] The unqualified phrase "recurve bow" or just "a recurve" in modern archery circles usually refers to a typical modern recurve bow, as used by archers in the Olympics and many other competitive events.

Recurve bows are more effective at close quarters. Test them in long ranged targets. That's because the arrow shot from the heavier straight bow will take longer to dissipate momentum.
You'll find that increasing the draw weight of a bow without increasing arrow weight quickly proves to be futile.  Arrow speed is most affected by the speed at which the limbs recoil, so limb material and design are the two most important factors. Lightweight arrows provide little resistance so poundage doesn't make must difference in the long run. A good example of this would be hitting a ping-pong ball with the intended paddle, then hitting it with a hammer and seeing if the ball flies faster. The ball won't speed up any noticeable amount. The hammer definitely has more force behind it (due to the hammer's greater mass than the paddle), but the ping pong ball provides so little resistance the force is wasted. In order to make that ping pong ball go faster you'll have to hit it with something travelling even faster than the paddle or hammer. However, if you want to accelerate a more massive object, like a brick, you'll find hitting it with a hammer provides a huge improvement over hitting it with a paddle. The same applies to archery, a more massive longbow can lob very heavy arrows at moderate velocities, and those arrows will penetrate very deeply do to their increased momentum. For a longbow like yours in the video, (70lbs of draw) i would suggest an 800-1200 grain arrow. For the recurve bow, something in the 400-600 grain range would probably be best. I have a hybrid "reflex-deflex" longbow that's 50lbs of draw (it closely resembles your recurve bow in practice) and it shoots 570 grain arrows very nicely. I also have a 70lb warbow, and with 500 grain arrows it's unimpressive for the effort to shoot it, however, with 1100 grain arrows it will shoot through concrete cider blocks. It's all about the physics.   Also, the spine (that is, how much the arrow flexes around the riser of the bow) for the arrow you're shooting is completely off. A properly spined arrow will penetrate much deeper due to more efficient use of energy. So you might look into getting a test kit and figuring out the best arrow spine for your bow. You'll be glad that you did.
