I believe the point of the recurve on that bow, and the reason it fires faster with a lower draw has to do with the points where the paracord is attached. That point is what I'm assuming is about 3 inches forward in relation to the cord's resting point because of the recurve, meaning those points (and the cord as well) have to travel a slightly longer distance than they normally would if the bow didn't have a recurve. And you know, Speed=distance/time, so by making the distance greater and keeping the time from the drawn state to the resting state the same, you're getting a faster arrow using a lower draw weight, just by increasing the distance the string needs to travel. While there is a direct correlation between draw weight and impact pressure a heavier draw does not necessarily mean more ft lbs of force go into the target. A heavier draw with a lighter arrow will move faster and have more effective range but it won't hit with as much force as a heavy arrow.

Your heavy bow has a much larger moment of inertia (swing weight) than the recurve bow. It has a heavier end, it is longer. (the weight of the end of a long limb has a much larger effect on swing weight). A lot of the energy stored in bending the bow is used to move the arms as opposed to the arrow. Additionally, a recurve bow in general has a number of benefits.  Recurve bows work by unbending each limb of the bow rather than by bending the whole bow more. Because of this, the force of the bow string on the limb is more forwards and backwards rather than up and down. This means that the limbs actually move less in order to provide the same draw as a long bow.   Because of this, when the string is released, less power is used to return the limbs to their start position than with a longbow. Additionally, I'm not certain, but I think a recurve bow might have a smoother power curve (ie the draw weight at different lengths does not change as much) This would mean that the string's push on the arrow does not decrease as much as it approaches its minimum position. Then again, you would have to measure the difference in draw weight for a very short draw in addition to a long draw.

Draw weight is draw weight when it comes to the physics of launching an arrow. The point of a recurve bow is that your able to get a higher draw weight in a more compact bow. Idk if the mongols invented the recurve but as far as I understand they were one of the first to use it, and the reason they favored it over a long bow is they were able to use it more effectively from horse back, which is how they preferred to fight. Most other people of the time used a long bow because they were easier to make and they either used them for hunting or a somewhat fixed position, so they didn't care to go through the extra processes to make them. I believe the reason your heavier long bow isnt getting as good of performance is due to your light arrow, generally lighter arrows have weaker splines. Basically the bow is over powering the arrow and the arrow is loosing energy due the flex. Do a quick youtube search on "Archers Paradox", there are some really good videos that explain it at depth.    Even a compound bow is only capable of shooting an arrow as fast as any other bow of the same weight, the reason compound bows are the most prevalent is that you are able to aim and stabilize a bow with a much higher draw weight due to the let off (there is also a bunch of fancy things you can do to tune them, and they're all "tacticool" looking so people buy them just cause). The recurve bow should have more power output for the same force at the draw, because of the force along the travel of the arrow. People tend to think the force required to keep the bow drawn is the force put into the arrow, but the bow is adding force all along the travel. You'll notice on your straight bows that the force required to keep pulling gets significantly harder as you go back. You have to add the force all along that path of travel to get the actual work put into the arrow. A lot of force applied when you first release, but very little is applied when the bow has almost reverted to its preferred shape.. The reason why your stopping point on the monster bow isn't earlier is precisely because of this increase in force required as the bow deforms.  So a normal bow needs an increase in force based on how far inward the ends are curving. The more perpendicular they are to the draw, the easier it is to pull. So to get more energy at the beginning of the pull that will multiply on top of your end draw strength, you need the curve inward of the ends to be closer to the curve inwards of the end when you have fully drawn the bow. The recurve accomplishes this quite well, since the end of where it is pulling has rotated around the tip.. It is also the same reason why a long bow has more power for the same draw length, because the extra length makes the ends curve less inward.
the design of the recurred bow allows the bows curves to be used to there full potential
when i looked at the two high power bows penetration, my immediate reaction was to blame it on arrows. you have a small bulge in the arrows tip, giving it a small penetrating head, but it avoids penetration (this is because it's not designed to penetrate into an animal but a wooden/rope/practice target), once you put enough force in to the arrow that thing ends up working pretty much the same way as non-Newtonian fluids. the more it has power the faster it will stop.  i.e. try the same thing with a broad head. if the heavier bow still doesn't penetrate, there's something else going on.
It's about speed of the limbs. A really heavy draw weight bow can shoot heavier arrows at same speed as a lighter draw weight bow shoots lighter arrows If their limbs are in proportion. The poundage is a bit like torque in cars.   To get more speed you should try to make the limbs lighter at the ends. I used wooden ends on my bows limbs (siyahs) and it's awesome how much more speed you can get. +180 fps or 55 m/s  It's also fun to experiment with different designs and see how they perform.  And PVC alone can give you easily over 150# as i managed to make a PVC prod for a crossbow at around 180#. It performed poorly in terms of speed though. only 35-40 m/s or 115-135 fps  This is just my take, please feel free to correct if I'm wrong!

Where did u find the fibreglass rods?
Speed of the arrow is proportional to the energy (work) put into bending the bow during the pull. The work is the sum of the products of draw weight x pull distance for each increment of pull (if you've had integral calculus, it's the integral of draw force as a function of draw distance, from zero to full draw). Best guess is that though the recurve does not reach the same maximum weight at full bend, if you graphed its draw weight vs draw distance you would see a steeper climb at the beginning of pull--it will get hard to pull after fewer inches, but then level off flatter. This means you are putting more total energy into the draw with the recurve--without having to hold the higher weight of the monster at max pull--and therefore the arrow travels faster with less perceived draw effort.  Sort of a low tech precursor to the let-off on modern compound bows, which is achieved with variable-radius cams. Pretty neat.

The recurves on the tips of the bow basically act as wrenches and allow you to store more energy in the bow while using less effort. It's all about the angle of the string to the limb of the bow.  If the angle is large, the string has less leverage and so the rate of draw weight increases greatly. If the angle is very acute, the string has more leverage, so it's easier to draw. This effectively increases the draw weight of a well made recurve bow, such that if it scales at lets say 45 lbs, it could equate to the shooting force of a 60+ lbs draw weight bow with straight limbs.
With the "monster" bow you should shorten the string. With bows it is usually the "rule of thumb" with the string and bow length, meaning you should be able to put a thumbs up between the string and the belly of the bow

The recurve works by opposing the direction of the string when you let go, during the last moments it would otherwise still hold the arrow. It stops the string before it can make a straight line of itself creating a snappier release and also dampens the vibration to the limbs.

HEY HOW ABOUT THE ARROW??
one way to think of the recurve bow is that aside from the badass look, it has a much smoother release so to speak. When you let go of the arrow, the 2 mini bows on the ends are absorbing the shock from the release and keeping that from slowing down the main power of the middle section of the bow.  your big bow, because it is so heavy and not properly narrowed on both ends (doesn't not get smaller as you reach the edges) like a normal long bow, the shock of the release due to the draw power is, almost for a slit second, halting the shot. but that's enough to make the difference. If you used a slightly heavier arrow or maid the ends lighter than the main body, you would remove or at least lessen this problem, depending on how good a job you do.Â    A good way to picture this is by imagining a heavy elastic pole of equal width from side to side. bend it far back and release. Because your ends are just as heavy, and in total heavier than your middle they are literally wobbling back and forth, (a slow mo video makes it very easy to see). this wobble is absorbing your kinetic energy, and if it was strong enough with a weak enough material, your bow would shatter.   Hope this helps.

A recurve bow stores more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an equivalent straight-limbed bow, giving a greater amount of energy and speed to the arrow. A recurve will permit a shorter bow than the simple straight limb bow for a given arrow energy and this form was often preferred by archers in environments where long weapons could be cumbersome, such as in brush and forest terrain, or while on horseback.

Those are nice.  A word of caution Too light an arrow(spine/stiffness) in too heavy a bow can cause the arrow to collapse and fold in two as you release it.  It also effects arrow launch and flight, so you want an arrow spine matched to the bow for consistent launch.  In the video where you launched the arrow past the camera, that it apears your knock point on the string in relation to where you are holding the bow and your finger as the arrow rest is a little off.  This causes the arrow to fly tail high or low.  A good way to gauge this is to make a wooden frame and tape a sheet of butcher paper or newspaper to it and put it a few feet in front of your target.  Shoot an arrow thru it and observe the pattern it tears in the paper.  Ideally it will be a center hole and 3 little tears from the fletching indicating a stable flying arrow. tail left or right is probably arrow spine mismatch, tail up or down is nock point/arrow rest position... Hi  So the reason why your recurve bow penetrates just as deep as your fibreglass bow is the energy stored/draw length graph.  The recurve shape is awesome since there is a more linear increase in this graph and stores the energy more linearly per unit of draw length of the bow.  Whereas the fibreglass bow "stacks" aka stores alot of energy in the beginning few units of draw length as compared to when you keep pulling on that bow and consequently stores less energy as you keep drawing for the later units of draw length. ( the graph is steep then levels off quickly)

from my experience the recurves extra bends gives the bow a greater pre load. a greater preload means that your max draw weight is reached much earlier in the draw. so when you release the string it has its maximum potentiometer almost the full length of travel all the way back to its resting position. where as a long bow style bow with no curves to create such a pre load you start to loose alot of potential energy before the sting ever travels it's full length of the draw. therefor the arrow looses punch if you will before it ever leaves the string. this is also why you will notice a shorter bow seeming to fire an arrow faster. this is just from my experience. first video of yours that I've watched. keep it up.

The recurve has a different draw force curve than the long bow.  If you were to graph the poundage on one side and the distance you draw the bow on the other, you would see that you have more energy being transferred to the arrow with the recurve because it holds the lighter draw weight longer than the long bow.  A standard hunting compound bow has a draw force curve  shaped like a upside down J so the peak weight is in the middle over a short distance then drops off at full draw.   Where the compound picks up so much more energy is it starts with low weight at the release of the arrow and builds speed before hitting peak weight causing the arrow to have forward momentum before it hits peak weight.  Simply put the recurve pushes the arrow longer with more weight even tho peak weight is less than your monster bow.

Good video! There are four things in my mind about the same performance of the two different bows. First is the draw length, if you can draw 40 pounds, but you can draw one bow further back with that force than the other the one with the longer draw will be stronger/faster. The reason is because it can accelerate the arrow in a longer distance. Second is simply the string, stiffer the string mean faster arrow (but too stiff string can damage the bow) Third is the actual weight of the bow (limbs), because the stored energy is accelerate you arrow but the same energie accelerate the limbs as the arrow as well so lighter bow with same drawweight means more efficient bow. Fourth is the characteristic of the bow. The drawweight is measured on 28 inches, but good bows hardens linearly, other bows like one of mine as well hardens suddenly in the end, and it can make it weaker than it feels. The reason is it can accelerate the arrow well only in the small fraction of the drawlenght.

ZednoughtAlpha the draw weight is the power of the bow, or the force behind the arrow. The elasticity of the bow determines the bows "snap", or the speed that the string travels. the better the snap, the quicker the arrow flies. more draw weight dose not mean faster, it just has more of an impact when it hits.  recurves in general are a high elasticity bow. they whip arrows because of the recurve design.  and great video by the way.

The Re-curve design manages to preserve the draw length (power stroke) of a much longer bow in a more compact design. That is why the Mongolians used them for mounted archery. Poundage is only half as important as then length of the draw. That is why despite crossbows averaging 300 lb pull weight, long bows shoot further and have better penetration even though they average much less at 115 lb (medieval war bow and war cross bow, modern bows usually have much less draw weight for hunting and practice shooting). The longer the draw, the more time the arrow has to accelerate before it leaves the spring. Remember that acceleration is more important than mass when calculating the energy of a projectile. Having a higher draw weight only benefits you if you can draw a full length. Even if you are at 80% the maximum draw length that can significantly change it's velocity. Think of it like when gunsmiths measure the muzzle velocity of a rifle vs a handgun. A longer barrel means the bullet inside has more time to accelerate. The exact same principle applies here.

There are two main reasons why the lighter recurve performs so well.  1. the recurve raises the acceleration of the arrow wuring the last bit of the drive, because when the string hits the recurve it shortens the string basically and that raises the speed of the string.  2. the higher the draw weight, the heavier the arrow has to be. A bow has a natural limit of possible arrow acceleration, if it gets near to it, a lot of energy is not transfered into the arrow (like hitting something that is already moving away from you), so the draw weight is wasted.  AND I do not know what you used to stuff the box, but for that kind of test you need a homogenous target (like foam) to be sure the resistance is consistent.

Eli y don't u post as often?
Recurve bows are, in essence, snappier than a straight bow. This is because you have a curve facing away from the draw. This gives more distance from the resting to the full draw points at the curve. As we see in springs, the more distance you push or pull the spring, the more energy is stored. And, for many springs, the rate itself is linear while the total work is more quadratic. This means that the bow can be shorter and have the same weight.  But why does it perform as well as the long bow? Like I mentioned, the bow is snappier due to the curve. If you know what impulse is, then you know that the impulse force has an inverse relationship with time. So, when your time is decreased, your force is increase. Since a recurve is faster than a straight bow, the impulse is much greater on the recurve.

zednought I did some research on a re curve and advantages to it here is what I found  A recurve bow has tips that curve away from the archer when the bow is unstrung. By definition, the difference between recurve and other bows is that the string touches a section of the limb when the bow is strung. A recurve bow stores more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an equivalent straight-limbed bow, giving a greater amount of energy and speed to the arrow. A recurve will permit a shorter bow than the simple straight limb bow for a given arrow energy and this form was often preferred by archers in environments where long weapons could be cumbersome, such as in brush and forest terrain, or while on horseback.

Yeah, the re curve bows, (In comparison to, say, a longbow would be the best analog for your monster bow in my eyes) generally have a higher initial velocity than a longbow with MUCH reduced draw weight, but don't shoot as far.  Because of this aspect, they were often used as horse bows, for use in horse archery, an example being the mongols, where range isn't really a factor, but draw weight and penetration power are.  That's just my personal views and knowledge attained through research and experience, you may have different opinions, I don't know. Recurve bows work off of a form of leverage, making them much more powerful than normal bows of the same size and material. Â This was to allow the hunter or bowman to use less energy drawing the arrow back so he could in theory shoot more arrows in the long run Or allow someone with a weaker arm to shoot arrows with the same effectiveness as someone with a stronger arm and normal bow. Â With all that said, once crossbows came into being, normal bowman started to die out. Â It takes very little effort and training to shoot a crossbow compared to using a bow of any shape or size.

The reason the recurve bow is more powerful is because when it is drawn, the limbs are bent back, when the arrow is released, the limbs become straight/slightly bent forward where as with a long bow, they go from bent back to less bent back but still bent back.

The better built a bow is, and the less unnecessary mass it has, the "faster" it will be. What this really means is that the bow is more efficiently using its draw weight. With a large diameter PVC bow, a lot of the force just goes into normalizing the PVC itself, making the arrow fly with less speed a force. The fiberglass helps this, but it will only do so much. With any recurve bow, the difference between strung and unstrung is quite substantial, meaning that there is already pressure on the bow, due to its structure, before you even pull the arrow back. Much more than a long bow, in most cases. So, 2 points, really. Firstly, unnecessary mass will make the bow fire more slowly. Secondly, the more tension in the bow before drawing the greater speed the arrow will have (with traditional bows, in most cases). I've made about 13 bows of different types and materials, and I was surprised when my 80b bundle bow hit with less authority then my 45b finished short bow. The more efficiently the bow is built, the less unnecessary mass, the better it will use its draw weight. Hope that was helpful/entertaining. I very much enjoy your videos and I wish you the best.

Hey Eli I'm the one who sent the bow. I was curious on the method you used to weigh them. Granted there are many factors that could affect the draw force.(namely my bad guessing) But if it has been shot fairly regularly it will break in, meaning it will get used to bending and flexing so it wont have as much resistance. my guess is that it was originaly 50-52 lbs and dropped to 45-48ish, just a theory. For the core i used three fiberglass rods one was 1/4in two were 3/16in. the 3/16 in was cut at 18in. ant the 1/4in was cut at 10in. then they were centered in the bow. *Recurve bow* 									 From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia 								 												 A recurve bow is a bow with limbs that curve away from the archer when unstrung. A recurve bow stores more energy and delivers energy more efficiently than an equivalent straight-limbed bow, giving a greater amount of energy and speed to the arrow. A recurve will permit a shorter bow than the simple straight limb bow for a given arrow energy and this form was often preferred by archers in environments where long weapons could be cumbersome, such as in brush and forest terrain, or while on horseback.
